Byrne looks in the mirror and finds nothing: &quot;And I  say to myself, 'Self, how did I get here?'&quot;  This album could have  been called &quot;Tales of a Funky Nothing.&quot;

B00002DE0S|5.0|This performance is the realization of a 20-year dream by conductor/soloist Zimerman, himself the 1975 Grand Prize winner of the International Chopin Competition in Warsaw. His dream was to tour and  perform the two concerti for the 150th anniversary of Chopin's death in  1849, with a hand-picked Polish-only orchestra. Nearly 400 audition tapes  were submitted, out of which he picked the very best 57 young performers. I  have heard them perform this program live at Mechanics Hall in Worcester  MA, as well as on CD, and both are incredible musical experiences. This  recording shows, far more than any other I've heard, Chopin's ardor for the  beautiful but unattainable young soprano Constancia Gladkowska.<br />The  playing is other-worldly. For example, in the second movement of the F  minor, the strings enter with a sound that appears to have no beginning, as  though it were there since the beginning of time. The result is  transporting. Whatever your age, you are 19 again (as Chopin was,) and  passionately, hopelessly in love with someone you cannot have.<br />Buy this  CD but before listening, make sure the shades are drawn, and that all cares  are set aside. This is a trip (don't we all wish it were a one-way trip!)  into the world of perfect, imagined love.

0385493622|4.0|Grandiose title aside, I found this to be an entertaining book. I particularly enjoyed reading about the various mathematical players throughout history who had an impact on Fermat's Last Theorem. I felt the  excitement of Wiles' announcement, almost as if I'd been there myself  during the event. And this was accomplished throughout without letting the  narrative get bogged down with heavy mathematics.<br />The Appendices were  very appropriate, in that they expanded on some mathematical concepts  mentioned in the text, while not distracting from the novel-like quality of  the story. I felt, however, that Singh did not go quite far enough with  this idea, when it comes to some of the higher mathematical concepts  ultimately employed in the solution of the Fermat problem. An appendix or  two giving the merest hint of what some of these techniques were about  would have been helpful.<br />I would also have welcomed some additional  speculation concerning whether or not Fermat actually did have a proof, as  he had claimed. Since Wiles' proof depended on such advanced 20th-century  mathematical techniques, obviously the 17th-century Fermat, if he did have  a proof, would have had a much different one. Singh devotes only three  short paragraphs to this fascinating question.<br />I enjoyed this book very  much. It makes me want to see the Nova program &quot;The Proof&quot;,  directed and coproduced by Singh.
6304504012|5.0|I saw this movie when it first came out and loved it, and just saw it again on video. This is one of the most under-rated films of the decade. Hollywood just doesn't  like Madonna, which resulted in this  wonderfully-acted film being unfairly snubbed by critics and the Oscar  establishment. Despite the presence of half the population of Argentina as  extras, this is essentially an intimate story that rides on the  performances of the three principals--Evita, Peron, and Che. Although  Madonna's voice isn't quite up to some of the vocal challenges, her  performance is otherwise on the mark. Antonio Banderas surprised me--his  somewhat gritty vocal quality is totally appropriate to his character as a  peasant revolutionary. Nobody smolders better. His feelings for Evita were  intriguingly ambiguous; it was fascinating to watch him veer from hope to  contempt to something close to sexual attraction. And Jonathan Pryce is one  of the premier musical theater artists of this era. What he does with the  rather underwritten part of Peron is a joy to see. Watch his performance of  &quot;She is a Diamond&quot;--two minutes of subtle, superb acting with  voice, face, body--absolutely wonderful. It seems that most of the  naysayers saw the show on Broadway and find the movie a poor comparison.  Each should be taken on their own terms. Patti LuPone is undoubtedly  stronger vocally than Madonna, but you have to give the latter credit for  taking on a huge challenge and (mostly) pulling it off. And I never thought  Mandy Patinkin's light tenor was right for Che--Banderas, though not half  the singer Patinkin is, has an earthy vocal quality that works better in  this role. I recommend this movie highly for all lovers of musical theater.

0684857820|5.0|I read Lucky on a flight home. I couldn't put it down. I continued reading it while waiting for my luggage in the baggage claim area and I did not leave the airport until I read the last page. My autonomic nervous system  took a beating. Alice's story haunts me. I think about the unintentional  damage we do to people who have suffered an act of violence when we attempt  to \\"understand.\\"  In our endeavor to put logic on the illogical, we tell  ourselves that what happened was their fault, perhaps because of weakness  or bad judgement.  When we convince ourselves of this, we convince  ourselves that we are in control our lives; random acts of violence  couldn't possibly happen to us.  Lucky blows apart this lie. Alice Sebold  won't let you hide behind rationalization, explanation, or theoretical  discussion on the ills of society to excuse or lighten the effects and the  damage an act of violence has on another person.  She will make you face  the random, violent, crazy world we live in with a writing style that is  direct, harsh and unapologetic. She never asks for your pity. But Lucky is  also a story written with compassion, humor, warmth and intelligence. As  Alice Sebold tells of her long and difficult journey from a place of fear,  rage and despair to a place of meaning and hope, you cannot help but be  humbled and awed at the complex union between fragility and strength in  human beings. Lucky is a book you will remember long after you have  finished the last page.

B0000025D0|5.0|First of all, let's set the record straight: &quot;Darkness&quot; is not better than born to run, it's just different.  Gone is the youthful naivete found on its predecessor; it has been replaced by a hard-won maturity and  acceptance.  This album says that life is tough, but life is not something  to run away from.  The answers are not just across the county line or in  some far-away fantasy world; they exist in the individual.  For the first  time we see Springsteen embracing reality, not trying to create some  fantastical universe where the guy gets the girl and they live happily ever  after.  In fact, I remember Bruce saying something about how in his  previous albums the guy always got the girl and they rode off into the  sunset...but this was the first album where he began to question,  &quot;Yeah, and then what?&quot;   Well, this album contains every kind of  &quot;then what&quot; you can imagine.  Throughout the album, Bruce  acknowledges the difficulty and pain we experience in life, but he says  that we must keep going (&quot;Badlands&quot;).  Going for what, you say?   Well, &quot;The Promised Land,&quot; of course; and in this song he says  that he believes that life is still worth living, despite shattered  illusions and broken dreams.  The people who give up are also a constant  force on this album, especially on songs like &quot;Racing in the  Street&quot;.  On this song, Bruce divides the world into two types of  people:  those who die a little bit more every day, and those who go home  every night, get dressed up, and go racing in the street.  Unlike the  characters in &quot;Born to Run,&quot; it doesn't even seem to matter if  they find the fulfillment they are looking for.  What matters is that they  continue to search.   I haven't even begun to talk about the music, either,  have I?  Well, the recording of this album ushers in a new Bruce; there is  no Wall of Sound, no jazzy, funky E Street Band jamming.  This album is  full of twisted guitar solos by Bruce, and it is the first time his guitar  skills are completely realized.  This may be the most &quot;guitar&quot;  album he's ever done.  But don't get me wrong: you still get the rest of  the E Street Band in full force: Max Weinberg and his thundering drums,  Clarence Clemons with that saxophone.  This album has a certain stark  quality to it; however, it is not nearly as stark and simplistic as later  efforts such as &quot;Nebraska&quot; and &quot;The Ghost of Tom Joad.&quot;   It's a great rock album, but it's not JUST a great rock album.  Bruce  become a philosopher here, the prophet we disciples followed into the next  decade with the release of its sequel, &quot;The River.&quot;  And if Bruce  is a prophet, this masterpiece of an album surely must be our guiding  light.     In many ways, &quot;Darkness&quot; is better than &quot;Born to  Run.&quot;  It surely is more textured and somehow throughout the record  Bruce establishes a connection with his listener like he never has before.   On previous albums, he played for us; on this album, he preaches and  instructs us.     Bruce Springsteen changed tremendously, both musically  and personally, between 1975 and 1978.  As he sings in &quot;The Promised  Land&quot;:  &quot;Mister, I ain't a boy, no, I'm a man, and I believe in  the Promised Land.&quot;  On this album, he takes us there.
B000001OAA|5.0|Shirley Manson is a great vocal talent.  Her songs are wry, witty, and hauntingly beautiful.  They talk to you using cyber-music, hard rock, and her amazing lyrics. 630170648X|5.0|I find it exasperating that so few of Ernst Lubitsch's films are currently available in either video or DVD.  By any reckoning, he is one of the few absolute masters of the cinema (he even makes Andrew Sarris's list of  Pantheon directors).  A few of his best films (this one, HEAVEN CAN WAIT,  NINOTCHKA) are in print, but several others are not, while lesser films  like ANGEL, BLUEBEARD'S EIGHTH WIFE, and THE SHOP AROUND THE CORNER are. 0385495315|3.0|This book has some deficiencies, in that it truly only talks about the breaking of the ENIGMA cipher from the British perspective, and does not pay tribute the incredible work of the Americans during WW2.<br />There are  new books on the market, such as one by David Kohnen of The Mariners'  Museum, which describe newly declassified US documentation about the  breaking of the 4-rotor German Naval Enigma machine, which the British  never really mastered. Singh takes the perspective, that without the  Bletchly Park operation, the US would not have successfully broken the  Enigma code, but that is just not entirely true. The more true statement  would include that the British turned over vital information early in the  war to the US, which assisted the US in creating faster and better BOMBE  machines, and that the advances made by the US were then never matched by  the British throughout the war.<br />I would consider this book as telling  only part of the story, and David Kahn's works on the subject as being much  more authoritative on the historical perspectives anyway. For more truthful  and factual information about the breaking of the 4-rotor Engima machine,  Kohnen's &quot;Winn and Knowles&quot; (published by Enigma Press in Poland,  1999) publication is clearly a better choice.

0790740443|4.0|The main attraction for buying the DVD is that given the low price one gets some very interesting added features, namely the audio commentary by the director and producer. Ultimately this is their film and it succeeds or  fails due to their influences. One of the great drawbacks is that the lead  character has very little dialogue with which to convey some complex  psychological issues. If not for the competent performance of Kurt Russell  as Sgt Todd (and the physical charms of Connie Nielsen) the film would fall  flat on its face. Notwithstanding, the action, special effects and  cinematography are sufficiently admirable to keep the sci-fi buffs  entertained. The movie begins on a good premise although I would question  the wisdom of placing the setting in the contemporary period when a more  futuristic treatment would seem more likely. But by far the greatest  deficiency originates with the lacklustre script, which is hard to  understand given thefact that the same pen which inked this mess wrote  Blade Runner. At some point it should have been realized that for the  audince to make a sypathetic connection with Sgt Todd he would have to  undergo an emotive transformation from a heartless killer. That aspect is  never successfully explored. Even Jason Isaacs declares on the audio  commentary that at times one screams out for Sgt Todd to say something but  he never progresses beyond the monosyllabic. One problem with the scenario  is that it seems pieced together from movie classics. The beginning evokes  memories of Conan the Barbarian then proceeds to emulate Shane while the  ending is clearly purloined from Rambo. There is no great shame in  modifying the works of others to suit ones own purpose, after all James  Cameron has become &quot;King of the World&quot; by utilizing this  approach. There is no forgiveness however for a poor script, especially  given the writer's credentials. I would have given this film three stars  out of five but due to the harshness of some previous reviews I have tried  to make ammends. With a slightly different treatment this movie could have  been a classic instead of standard fare. Nevertheless, the audio commentary  for this DVD is quite revealing about the film making process and is worth  the price of purchase.

He is unscrupulous in starting his new  business and yet discovers his own humanity, to the surprise of himself as  well as his friends and family. 0553108034|5.0|Of all the fantasy series on the market, only three, to me, combine page-turning plotlines, characters you could cry over, solid writing--and believable worlds. Martin joins Robert Jordan, Tad Williams, and early  Katharine Kerr as the best of what modern fantasy has to offer. I know how  popular Salvatore, Eddings, Feist, et al. are; but read them again, and  what you get is good story with junior high school-level writing ability.  Terry Goodkind spins a good yarn, but he's too derivative of Robert Jordan  (why no copyright infringement lawsuit?) and his writing is average at  best. Martin's series, though... wow. Tad Williams remains, to me, the  best-written of all series, Jordan's plot is perhaps the most captivating,  and early Kerr was so real you could smell the horse and steel. Martin is  not the best in any one area, but he does it all so well you'll find  yourself staying up all night turning pages. My only anger is the year-long  wait until the next installment. I know some will chafe at the fact that  this second volume is mostly build-up, with just about nothing getting  resolved. But what a build-up; the tension gets so thick you want to scream  at the characters to get a clue and see what's really coming. If your idea  of fantasy is two-hundred pages of improbably-named Dungeons and Dragons  cliches killing monsters plagiarized from Tolkien, go back to your  Salvatores. But if you're into believable worlds crafted by authors who  obviously have done some homework into late medieval history to flesh out  the details, and characters who are _human_, with human failings, do  yourself a favor and buy A Game of Thrones and A Clash of Kings. Better  yet, wait until the series is done before you start, or you'll find  yourself in agony, like I am now.

B000001FS3|5.0|Too much has already been written concerning the technical aspects of the  juxtaposition of music, production, and lyrics on The Joshua Tree.  More significant to the album is the way that with every song, and indeed,  through the course of the whole of the album, it seems U2 has attempted to  establish a sense of both physical and emotional place through the use of  the aforementioned elements, an attempt which has clearly been satisfied,  giving the listener a clear sense of the grandeur, urgency, and beauty that  characterize the landscapes they have attempted to create.  I have not  heard another album which has been able to vividly and effectively evoke  recurrent images and emotions with both desperation and grace the way The  Joshua Tree has.   Listen to it while driving through the desert at night,  or in the middle of the city.  It doesn't matter what your location is.    This album not only seems to add poignancy to your locale, it takes you  elsewhere, to a place where things seem to matter more, and where life  takes on the sort of urgent reality that it ought to always have.  Good  music, in any form, can either make you forget your cares by taking you  away from them, or can somehow give voice to or characterize those cares.   Rarely is it possible for music to combine the two, but with The Joshua  Tree, U2 has created an album which removes the listener from their mundane  surroundings, and yet will not allow them to forget so many of the very  issues which exist at the heart of our human experience.  This in mind, The  Joshua Tree is ultimately an album with depth, atmosphere, and passion  unparalleled.

There is something sinister, even  supernatural, going only here.  Blake's man in black is entirely scary,  something straight out of a nightmare.  That party scene with the cellphone  where Blake is two places at once is very disturbing.  At first I wondered  if Blake was merely a figment of Fred's imagination, but ruled this out  because of the shot of Blake talking with another party-goer shortly  afterwards, and of his later interaction with characters like Loggia.   Consider Blake's monstrous appearance.  The character even looks  diabolical.  Deathly pale, with these evil black eyes.  He doesn't look  human at all, he looks dead (and I did toy with the idea of his being a  personification of Death)--and devilish.  He has this creepy dialogue about  meeting Pullman before and having &quot;been called&quot;... it had this  Faustian aura to it, like Pullman had made some devil's bargain and then  forgotten about his end of the deal.  (Conversely, I can see how some  viewers would, instead, see Blake as a mental evil alter ego of Pullman's  murderous side.)  <br />Patricia Arquette's character is like some sultry  seductress, ensuring Pullman's (and Getty's) damnation.  Notice that really  quick shot of her face at the beginning, before Pullman turns on the light,  when she is actually Blake!<br />When Pullman morphs into Getty in the prison,  I kept wondering if the movie was about &quot;lost souls&quot; and if both  Pullman and Getty were pawns of the mephistophelian Blake.  (I neverconsidered that Pullman and Getty were one and the same person.  Pullman is  too cultered, too artistic, to be the hoodlum Getty--who also is very  disturbed by Pullman's sax music)  Notice that Getty's character is,  initially, quite dazed--as though he has returned from the dead-- and he  has a wound on his forehead suspiciously like a bullet hole (this  disappears over time).  Getty's parents avoid discussing what &quot;has  happened&quot; to Getty--tears in their eyes--and I immediately figured  that Getty had died horribly and been returned to them...  The parents are  so grateful to just have him back (notice how blase they are about his  choice of friends and all-night outtings)that they don't question anything.   Getty's character is also told by Blake that they have &quot;met  before.&quot;  Again, I wondered if this were a Faustian pact.  In the end,  Pullman returns, ever at the mercy of a persuing Blake (tormenting him with  videocam) and flees, futilely down an unending highway into damnation (?)  or insanity.  His face deforms as he screams, as though a mixture of all  the lost souls, or of his body switching rapidly from Pullman to Getty and  back?  Or, is it all just insanity?  <br />Does it really matter?  <br />The  whole atmosphere of the film (and its mind-blowing full-circle ending)is  remarkable and disturbing.  There is some great humor too in the film  involving Loggia (the road-rage scene, and Loggia's eerie phone call to  Getty, &quot;I'm glad you're doing fine...&quot;)  Damn creepy.   <br />&quot;Lost Highway&quot; is a must-see.  I still don't have all the  answers and I know they'll never come.  But it's the mood and eerie visual  images (the reverse shot of the exploding cabin, like a premonition)that  make this film so brilliant (and some awesome music tracks).  This film  will haunt you for years to come.

B000002KON|5.0|It is ironic that &quot;Arthur, or the Decline &amp; Fall of the British Empire&quot; may be the Kinks greatest album, and is also their last great one. The Kinks albums after this (beginning with &quot;Lola&quot;) all have  chestnuts, but they still represent the &quot;decline &amp; fall of the  kinks&quot;. The music on &quot;Arthur&quot; was looser and  &quot;stoned&quot;. The songs were getting longer. Dave Davies guitar  accompaniment to tunes like &quot;Yes Sir, No Sir&quot; &amp; &quot;Winston  Churchill Says&quot; are free-form noodles. However, sort through these  less attractive elements, and you'll find the best songs Ray Davies ever  wrote: &quot;Shangri La&quot;, &quot;Driving&quot;, &quot;She's Bought a  Hat Like Princess Marina&quot;, and &quot;Some Mother's Son&quot;. And  despite the occassional forays into &quot;typical late 60's music&quot;,  this collection of songs demonstrates Ray Davies intelligence and  independence. He may have been a &quot;rock star&quot; at this point (a  roll he really embraced in the 70's to embarrassing effect), but he seemed  to eschew the trappings of pop culture, fashion, &amp; self-importance.  <br />&quot;Arthur&quot; shows us (for the last time) Ray Davies as a  sensitive and wise young man who cherished tradition and the collapsed  world of the British Empire. These songs show him struggling to find  meaning and direction in a world where idealism and innocence are lost (as  he lamented on the &quot;Village Green&quot; album). It is sad to consider  that the only solution which seemed to occur to him was escapism, as in  &quot;Driving&quot;. Of course, this is not a judgement on Ray Davies. In  fact, just look how far we've drifted into isolation and complacence in the  intervening years...

Unfortunately such contradictions seem inherent because so many different authors are contributing to the same basic story--Dragonlance.  But don't take that as a deterrent to reading THE CHRONICLES; the original trilogy truly is a gem.  I also recommend THE LEGENDS, which is also written by Margaret Weis and Tracy Hickman--actually anything they write is worth checking out.  It makes sense that as the creators and genius behind Dragonlance, they can do it best.

0393317552|2.0|In the 1980's film \\"Trading Places\\" Eddie Murphy andDan Akroyd traded places as part of a $1 bet, made by two owners of a commodities trading firm.  One bet that good commodities brokers were born and bred, genetically, so to speak, while the other, equally vociferously argued that it was only training that counted and that genetics and breeding counted for nothing.  The argument was nature vs. nurture and, being the 80's, nurture naturally won.     Jared Diamond's book is to world history what Trading Places was to the nature/nurture debate---a well thought out but highly subjective treatment.  Maybe someday we will be able to dispassionately discuss the issue, but Guns, Germs, and Steel is not the place to get that treatment.     The book is as skewed to the belief that only environmental factors affect success---the society with the most varities of domesticatable plants and animals will, over time, prevail over all other societies---as say The Myth of the 20th Century, the infamous guide to Nazism that pre-dated Mein Kampf, is skewed toward the belief that only genetics determines success. Both are well documented and passionately argued, and equally oversimplified.     Read it, by all means, but don't necessarily believe it all.  The book has its strengths---its historical accounts of Pizarro's conquest of South America is fascinating.  But some of his theories, and remember, this is all theory, are weak.

B00005GL0Q|5.0|Arguably The Beatles best album, \\"Revolver\\" continues to sparkle with innovation, great melodies and sharp lyrics forty years on. From the moment that \\"Taxman\\" demands your attention with the count-in on to the bizarre seagull/laughing sounds (that's a tape loop distorted of Paul McCartney laughing) that open \\"Tomorrow Never Knows\\" the album features solid slabs of experimental brilliant rock 'n' roll by one of the best and most popular bands of the 20th century.<br /><br />Harrison's \\"Taxman\\" with a brilliant rapid fire machine gun guitar solo from McCartney opens the album. McCartney's \\"Eleanor Rigby\\" continues to be powerful with its strident string arrangement (producer George Martin and composer McCartney were inspired to write the string arrangement by Bernard Herrmann's score for Francois Truffaut's film \\"Farenheit 451\\")comes on stage next with a completely different sound and texture. \\"I'm Only Sleeping\\" (which wasn't on the album in the U.S. originally but on \\"Yesterday and Today\\")has a lilting melody and laidback delivery by Lennon. Harrison dives into Indian music with \\"Love You To\\" grafting sitars and tamboras to a western melody. \\"Here, There and Everywhere\\" received the ultimate compliment when Lennon stated he wished he had written it (it's McCartney's completely). It's a masterful love song with a brilliant, rich melody. \\"Yellow Submarine\\" gives Ringo the spotlight in a children's song that with all of its silliness acts as a perfect counterpoint to Lennon's closer \\"She Said, She Said\\" inspired by Peter Fonda's inane comments to Lennon.<br /><br />The second side of the album opened with \\"Good Day Sunshine\\" a bright melodic song by McCartney inspired by The Lovin' Spoonful's \\"Daydream\\". It's still a great inspirational song to help you greet the day. Lennon's \\"And Your Bird Can Sing\\" was slighted by the composer in many interviews but the dual guitar attack riff played by Harrison and Lennon is great. \\"For No One\\" has Paul digging into his own psyche (inspired by his relationship with Jane Asher)it's a slice of life with a brilliant horn solo by Alan Civil. \\"Doctor Robert\\" written about a pill pusher the band knew that could make you right drips with sarcasm. The middle eight with its unusual changes is an example of what made the Lennon-McCartney songwriting team so brilliant. Often slighted \\"I Want To Tell You\\" features Harrison's off-beat melody and experimental vocal arrangements at a pinnacle. McCartney continues to try on every style popular at the time excelling at his songcraft with the 60's R&B inspired \\"Got To Get You Into My Life\\". Finally the album closes with Lennon's inspired drone \\"Tomorrow Never Knows\\" (originally called \\"The Void\\" but renamed by Ringo with his typical wit)which consists of one chord and Lennon's voice sounding like he's a million miles away. The use of tape loops here was groundbreaking and powerful for pop music at the time. Engineer Geoff Emerick found the perfect balance between experimental sound and pop music when he engineered this track pulling pushing his creative engineering to the limits.<br /><br />While album is in desperate need of a fine remaster (the releases of \\"Yellow Submarine Songtrack\\" and \\"Love\\" show just how sharp these songs could be on CD) the album continues to be brilliant. The only recent album that can come close to this one in my mind is U2's \\"Achtung Baby\\" which also combined musical styles in a blender for a unique sound.  Highly recommended.is relationship with Jane Asher)it's a slice of life with a brilliant horn solo by Alan Civil. \\"Doctor Robert\\" written about a pill pusher the band knew that could make you right drips with sarcasm. B00000AFFF|3.0|It's one thing to admire and emulate your influences; it's another to give yourself over to them so completely that you come off sounding hollow by comparison. Elvis Costello works best whenever he uses his  influences--whether they're George Jones, Motown, The Beatles or Burt  Bacharach--as starting points from which he adds his own unique qualities.  KING OF AMERICA, IMPERIAL BEDROOM, GET HAPPY and SPIKE were all albums that  showed Costello drawing inspiration from those and other role models and  forging some new art from them. But then you get albums like ALMOST BLUE or  PAINTED FROM MEMORY where he so slavishly tries to re-create a style and  ends up with a feeble pastiche. The songs here might well be as classic as  the ones Bacharach wrote with Hal David, but you'd never know it from  Costello's overwrought attempts to be Dionne Warwick whenever he sings.  Since when did Dusty Springfield, Mel Torme or Karen Carpenter attempt to  mimic Warwick whenever they tackled Bacharach? I think there's probably  more here than meets the ear, but I hope that, should this songwriting  partnership gets another outing, Costello will loosen up enough to sound  like himself instead of who he THINKS he should sound like.

Pepper\\" one of The Beatles seminal albums (when they transitioned forever from as Bob Dylan stated \\"being cute\\" to being \\"taken seriously\\")sounds quite nice. Pepper\\" into the 21st century.<br /><br />The bass is fuller, smoother and rounder sounding capturing what McCartney achieved on the mastertapes with help from engineer Geoff Emerick. Detail is strong although I will admit that because of some peak limiting some audiophiles will be put off by this edition.<br /><br />Imitation is the sincerest form of flattery. When Mick Jagger  and Keith Richard(s) heard Pepper they immediately decided they needed to  try something similiar (hence Satanic Majesties). By the time TSMR came out  the Beatles had moved on. Just after the release of TSMR there's a story  that McCartney played Jagger and Richard(s) their next single (Hey  Jude/Revolution) and both were knocked out by the single. They immediately  sensed they needed to return to their roots just as the Beatles had done.  Anyone that doubts the band's musical ability needs to relisten to the  band's early albums which, for all intents and purposes, were recorded live  (they only had four track machines folks). Later, the band themselves  admitted the circus of touring and 20 minute concerts dimished their  playing live, but even on as unadnored an album as The Beatles (aka The  White Album), one can hear the band with little or no overdubbing.<br /><br />Pepper  had a huge cultural and musical impact. B000002UAR|5.0|First, I think it is absurd to say that any Beatles album is their &quot;best.&quot; From Please Please Me through Abbey Road, every album is a classic document of where they were at the time (with the obvious  exception of the Yellow Submarine soundtrack, which shouldn't even be  considered a proper Beatles album). I love Revolver, but there is a haze  around it. The great thing about the Beatles is that they never took  themselves too seriously and could always inject good-natured humor into  there work. It's always irritating when this song, Obladi-oblada, Maxwell's  Silver Hammer, etc. get lambasted by people. Maybe Yellow Submarine would  have been more in context as a separate single, but this is the way we have  it, and I still think it's a brilliant ditty, bringing a little world of  its own to life, and giving Lennon a chance to be Lennon. I wouldn't trade  it for 20 Cold Turkeys or 55 Silly Love Songs.<br />All right - this album  does contain some of Lennon/McCartney's best songwriting, some of the  Beatles' best ensemble playing, some of George Martin's best production  (though their production masterpieces are still Pepper and Abbey Road.)  <br />Brilliant, but Rubber Soul and Abbey Road, though arguably less  innovative, are even more brilliant.

Not only is it great to dance to, but my  3-month-old daughter is fascinated by it!

So what can I say to Bob Dylan for his three  offerings of Christian music--&quot;Slow Train Coming,&quot;  &quot;Saved,&quot; and &quot;Shot of Love&quot;?  Simply this.  Thanks.  I  needed that, and still do.
B00002MZ4W|2.0|I'm one of those that was totally entranced by &quot;Tidal&quot; when it came out; the sensous bad-girl-who-thinks-too-much growling on that cd along with some innovative song-writing made me listen to that cd over and  over. This one didn't exactly jump out at me with anything new. The beats  and harmonies are pretty much standard and the song-writing isn't as  innovatively transcendent. Yet her lyrics are still profoundly wicked and  vulnerable. Still though, without the musical &quot;edge&quot; that Tidal  had , this one just didn't get my cd player repeat performance over and  over...
